  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_384b2cc5-87e5-4049-a0a8-9801f165bd00'

<span id="freeTextContainer8305589449855211840">"Good deal of fog this morning. I do not go out in the fog myself," notes Adam in his diary, adding, "The new creature does. It goes out in all weathers. And talks. It used to be so pleasant and quiet here." <br/>Adam has a lot to learn about Eve, and even more from her, as she names the animals, discovers fire, and introduces all manner of innovations to their garden home. Mar</span>
  <span id="freeText8305589449855211840" style="display:none">"Good deal of fog this morning. I do not go out in the fog myself," notes Adam in his diary, adding, "The new creature does. It goes out in all weathers. And talks. It used to be so pleasant and quiet here." <br />Adam has a lot to learn about Eve, and even more from her, as she names the animals, discovers fire, and introduces all manner of innovations to their garden home. Mark Twain's "translation" of the diaries of the first man and woman offers a humorous "he said/she said" narrative of biblical events. The great American storyteller found comfort and inspiration in the company of women, and his irreverent look at conventional religion is also a thoughtful -- and humorous -- argument for gender equality.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2506707554385928308">The Diaries of Adam and Eve, Mark Twain<br/><br/>Mark Twain retells the Garden of Eden story in the first person, presenting the separate perspectives in diary form of the legendary father and mother of the human race. <br/><br/>In Twain's version Adam comes across as the original couch potato, grumpily uninterested at first in the new long-haired creature with the different shape who keeps pestering him with her new ideas. Eve, by contrast, is the talkative, ever-curious experimenter whose inquisitive nature promp</span>
  <span id="freeText2506707554385928308" style="display:none">The Diaries of Adam and Eve, Mark Twain<br /><br />Mark Twain retells the Garden of Eden story in the first person, presenting the separate perspectives in diary form of the legendary father and mother of the human race. <br /><br />In Twain's version Adam comes across as the original couch potato, grumpily uninterested at first in the new long-haired creature with the different shape who keeps pestering him with her new ideas. Eve, by contrast, is the talkative, ever-curious experimenter whose inquisitive nature prompts her to name all the animals in the garden and leads her to the discovery of fire, among other things. <br /><br />Cain and Abel also make brief appearances as squawking infants whom Adam largely ignores while Eve does all the hard work of child-rearing. It doesn't dawn on him till they are almost full-grown that these puzzling new creatures are his children.This is a funny and touching retelling of the age-old myth, full of Twain's sarcastic humor, incisive intelligence, and subtle touches of pathos for the foibles of human nature.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و دوم ماه ژوئن سال 1998 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: خاطرات آدم و حوا؛ نویسنده: مارک تواین؛ مترجم: حسن علیشیری؛ تهران، دارینوش، 1384؛ در 87ص؛ چاپ سم 1389؛ شابک 9789645250513؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان امریکایی - سده 19م<br /><br />عنوان: دفتر خاطرات آدم و حوا : مجموعه داستانهای کوتاه مارک تواین؛ نویسنده: مارک تواین؛ مترجم: سعید بافندی؛ تهران، جامی، 1391؛ در 208ص؛ شابک 9786001760553؛<br /><br />روایت نخستین داستان عاشقانه دنیا است، که مارک تواین به زیبایی و طنازی آنرا نوشته است<br /><br />نقل از کتاب خاطرات آدم و حوا: حوا: سه شنبه، چهارشنبه، پنجشنبه و امروز: همه بدون دیدن اون. زمان زیادیه واسه تنها موندن. اما با این حال تنها بودن از اینکه حس کنی مزاحمی و نمیخوانت بهتره. باید یه همدم داشته باشم. فکر میکنم برای این کار ساخته شدم، واسه همین با حیوونا دوست میشم. اونا هم جذابن هم مودب و مهربون. هیچ وقت عنق نیستن و نمیذارن حس کنی مزاحمی. بهت لبخند می زنن و برات دم تکون میدن البته اگه دم داشته باشن. همیشه هم آماده بازی و سر و صدا کردن و اینور اونور گشتن یا هر کار دیگه ای که بگی هستن. ما با هم خیلی جاها رو گشتیم. بیشتر جاهای دنیا رو دیدم، شایدم همه دنیا رو. پس من اولین جهانگرد دنیام. اولین و تنها جهانگرد دنیا.؛ پرنده ها و حیوونا همه باهم دوستن و هیچ وقت باهم بحث و دعوا نمیکنن. اونا باهم حرف میزنن. با منم حرف میزنن. اما احتمالا به یه زبون خارجی صحبت میکنن چون من حتا یک کلمه از حرفاشون رو نمیفهمم. با این حال وقتی من باهاشون حرف میزنم میفهمند چی میگم، مخصوصا سگ و فیل. این باعث خجالت منه، چون نشون میده از من با هوشترن، بنابراین نسبت به من برتری دارن. این من رو اذیت میکنه چون میخوام فقط خودم تجربه اصلی باشم.؛<br /><br />برگزیده هایی از متن<br />حوا: این دنیای نوساز و بزرگ قشنگترین اثر هنریه<br />حوا: گاهی از خودم میپرسم این موجود واقعا به چه دردی میخوره؟ اصلا قلب داره؟<br />حوا: خیلی کم حرف میزنه. شاید چون باهوش نیست و به این مسئله حساسه و میخواد پنهونش کنه<br />آدم: به من گفت از یه دنده من که از بدنم گرفته شده، ساختنش. حرفش یکم مشکوکه، چون همه دنده هام سر جاشون<br />آدم: او همراه خوبیه و میدونم اگه نبود، خیلی تنها و افسرده میشدم<br />آدم: زندگی کردن بیرون از بهشت، اما با حوا، خیلی بهتر از زندگی کردن تو بهشت، اما بدونه اونه<br />آدم: واسه اینکه زیر بارون نمونم یه سر پناه ساختم، اما اونجا هم نتونستم آرامش داشته باشم... اون موجود جدید (حوا) مزاحم شد و وقتی سعی کردم بیرونش کنم، از سوراخهایی که باهاش میبینه آب بیرون میومد، و اون با پشت پنجه هاش پاکشون میکرد و از خودش صدایی رو درمیآورد که حیونا وقتی ناراحتن درمیآرن.؛ پایان نقل<br /><br />حوا: قبل از این، انقدر به همه ی چیزهای قشنگ علاقه داشتم، که مثل گیجا، فقط دستمو طرفشون دراز میکردم. بعضی وقتها خیلی دور بودن، و بعضی وقتها چند سانتیمتر باهام فاصله داشتن. اما من فکر میکردم چند متر ازم دورن، خیلی وقتا کلی هم خار تو این فاصله بود! اینطوری یه درسی رو یاد گرفتم، در ضمن واسه خودم یه قانون ساختم: اولین قانون من: «یک تجربۀ زخمی از خار دوری میکند»... گمونم واسه کسی به سن و سال من، نتیجه گیری خوبیه. پایان نقل.؛<br />ا. شربیانی</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5242867489964031833">The first half of this short book was absolutely excellent. Eve is a free spirit determined to enjoy and bring order to the wonderful Garden of Eden. Adam is a curmudgeonly misogynist who thinks Eve talks too much and should give all this naming of animals and plants a rest. <br/><br/>The second half of this book is throw-across-the-room frustrating and annoying. The scales fall from their eyes, Eve fancies Adam like mad and falls in love with him. They marry and forever after she subsumes her individuali</span>
  <span id="freeText5242867489964031833" style="display:none">The first half of this short book was absolutely excellent. Eve is a free spirit determined to enjoy and bring order to the wonderful Garden of Eden. Adam is a curmudgeonly misogynist who thinks Eve talks too much and should give all this naming of animals and plants a rest. <br /><br />The second half of this book is throw-across-the-room frustrating and annoying. The scales fall from their eyes, Eve fancies Adam like mad and falls in love with him. They marry and forever after she subsumes her individuality completely, and is absolutely dedicated to being an entirely submissive good wife to Adam, even willing to die for him. Which, according to Twain, together with motherhood, is the absolute zenith of a woman's life.<br /><br />I'm sure the many male-created, run and dominated fundamentalist religions of the world would absolutely agree with him and maybe should substitute this book for Genesis. </span>

<div id="review_1096306956" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1096306956" />
    <a title="Caroline " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3236194-caroline"><img alt="Caroline " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576541068p2/3236194.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1096306956?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline " href="/user/show/3236194-caroline">Caroline </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781573928274" href="/book/show/99146.The_Diaries_of_Adam_and_Eve">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=memorable-characters">memorable-characters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=short-and-quick">short-and-quick</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=page-turner">page-turner</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1096306956" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15746983370432253695">***NO SPOILERS***<br/><br/>No fan of Mark Twain’s work should overlook this slim masterpiece, an ingenious and highly amusing peek into the minds and lives of Adam and Eve during their first days. The narrative is organized as half Adam’s diary and half Eve’s. The tones are distinct. Adam’s is a straightforward, puzzled, and sometimes cranky daily accounting of the day’s accomplishments and discoveries. He shuns Eve and complains about her to hilarious effect. Eve’s is optimistic, wonder-struck, and feeli</span>
  <span id="freeText15746983370432253695" style="display:none">***NO SPOILERS***<br><br>No fan of Mark Twain’s work should overlook this slim masterpiece, an ingenious and highly amusing peek into the minds and lives of Adam and Eve during their first days. The narrative is organized as half Adam’s diary and half Eve’s. The tones are distinct. Adam’s is a straightforward, puzzled, and sometimes cranky daily accounting of the day’s accomplishments and discoveries. He shuns Eve and complains about her to hilarious effect. Eve’s is optimistic, wonder-struck, and feeling. She seeks out Adam and is depressed when she doesn’t spot him, sometimes for days at a time. Both diaries have in common a deep curiosity and sense of awe, and both are truly laugh-out-loud comedic in their own ways. Eve’s in particular is moving at times. <br><br>It’s difficult to decide what’s best about <i>The Diaries of Adam and Eve</i>. It’s playful and fascinating from start to finish, proof that a novella can be just as engaging and memorable as any Pulitzer-Prize౼winner. The story is one giant quotable passage (see status updates below this review), and little observations Eve records about Adam (and vice versa) make it startlingly clear just how fresh her perceptions are (e.g., at one point she notes that his body is shaped like a carrot). <br><br>Readers will get a special kick out of the illustrations, which complement the story perfectly. In this edition, Adam’s are depicted as his own, coarsely drawn on slabs of stone, and most are funny: <a href="http://imgur.com/2T1SKio" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1420046604i/13159395._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/i6ijc23" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1420046604i/13159396._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br>Eve’s are from a third-person perspective and lovely: <a href="http://imgur.com/MTrGKSD" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1420046604i/13159397._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><a href="http://imgur.com/7jld23g" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1420046604i/13159398._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br>Strangely, some versions of this book don't contain illustrations. Readers should seek out an illustrated edition, as the illustrations greatly enhance the writing and compose half the story. Each story page has an illustration opposite, and some story pages contain only a sentence or two, in plain writing. The book easily could be read in a single setting, in just a few minutes. (It should be noted that a basic knowledge of Genesis--Twain alludes to the part of Genesis that mentions Eve was in charge of naming the animals, for instance--is helpful but not necessary.)<br><br><i>The Diaries of Adam and Eve</i> is priceless. It contains Twain’s trademark humor, wit, and inventiveness and plays up differences so astutely that one cannot deny just how sharply observant he was in his study of people and how they relate to one another. <br><br>Final verdict: A first-rate, unforgettable novella. Readers looking for something short, funny, and a little touching should grab this now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15746983370432253695" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1166773510" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1166773510" />
    <a title="Riku Sayuj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1651956-riku-sayuj"><img alt="Riku Sayuj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1433433464p2/1651956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1166773510?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Riku Sayuj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Riku Sayuj" href="/user/show/1651956-riku-sayuj">Riku Sayuj</a>
      </span>

          marked it as to-read




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1166773510" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12155200262376866087"><br/><b>Expulsion from The Garden of Eden, Reinterpreted</b><br/><br/>We take upon ourselves the power of Judgment, without the power of Discernment. This is the message of the Expulsion story:<br/><br/>Consider Hobbes' discussion of Genesis:<br/><br/><i>"[We can look at] “You shall be as gods, knowing good and evil.”(Genesis, 3. 5) And, “Who told thee that thou wast naked? Hast thou eaten of the tree, of which I commanded thee thou shouldest not eat?”(Genesis, 3. 11) <br/><br/>For the cognizance or judicature of good and evil, being forbidden by th</i></span>
  <span id="freeText12155200262376866087" style="display:none"><br /><b>Expulsion from The Garden of Eden, Reinterpreted</b><br /><br />We take upon ourselves the power of Judgment, without the power of Discernment. This is the message of the Expulsion story:<br /><br />Consider Hobbes' discussion of Genesis:<br /><br /><i>"[We can look at] “You shall be as gods, knowing good and evil.”(Genesis, 3. 5) And, “Who told thee that thou wast naked? Hast thou eaten of the tree, of which I commanded thee thou shouldest not eat?”(Genesis, 3. 11) <br /><br />For the cognizance or judicature of good and evil, being forbidden by the name of the fruit of the tree of knowledge, as a trial of Adam’s obedience, the devil to inflame the ambition of the woman, to whom that fruit already seemed beautiful, told her that by tasting it they should be as gods, knowing good and evil. <br /><br />Whereupon having both eaten, they did indeed take upon them God’s office, which is judicature of good and evil, but acquired no new ability to distinguish between them aright."</i><br /><br /><b>My Take: The Idiotic Species</b><br /><br />God was just having fun with Adam. The fruit was nothing special. God just told them that it will give them the power of knowing Good and Evil -- to tempt them, to see if they can resist: After all, any intelligent dude will know that mortals can never distinguish Good and Evil!<br /><br />But they bought it! And ate of the fruit. God was disappointed that his creation turned out to be such a doofus species. He banished them and forgot about them.<br /><br />So they left, abandoned by God. But they still believed they had the power of discerning Good and Evil and used that misunderstanding to guide them.<br /><br />They have been fighting wars ever since, killing and exploiting each other, always convinced their own side, their own way of doing things, their own way of looking at things, etc. is "Good', and the other "Evil".<br /><br />God, when he cares to have look at this corner of the universe, still shakes his head in disbelief. No other species in the entire universe had failed that simple test, you know. We were the only dropouts.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12155200262376866087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3249160892" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13541639649036645353">I found this to be mundane.<br/><br/>Aside from being a parody of “Adam and Eve” Bible version I couldn’t really see what the purpose was. Maybe, back in the day, this was considered controversial. Having grown-up with Monty Python and “Life of Brian”, Twain’s writing is rather meek. Eve is a caricature female – caring, talkative, loving flowers, animals… Adam is a knucklehead.<br/><br/>It’s a short book, otherwise I would not have completed it.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_46205709" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/46205709" />
    <a title="Jared" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2027169-jared"><img alt="Jared" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1234569349p2/2027169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/46205709?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jared" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jared" href="/user/show/2027169-jared">Jared</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780965881159" href="/book/show/158951.Diaries_of_Adam_Eve">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2027169-jared?shelf=taught">taught</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer46205709" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7306093333078024045">While Twain plays on and derives humor from some familiar gender stereotypes, this is in no way a one-joke piece. If anything, the book’s “gimmick”--the translation of previously undiscovered texts direct from the Garden of Eden--provides it with the fuel for genius. The man and the woman, while playing their traditional, stereotypical roles to perfection, also happen to be the first of their sexes. As a result, Adam and Eve are able to play out the battle of the sexes unhampered by the cultural</span>
  <span id="freeText7306093333078024045" style="display:none">While Twain plays on and derives humor from some familiar gender stereotypes, this is in no way a one-joke piece. If anything, the book’s “gimmick”--the translation of previously undiscovered texts direct from the Garden of Eden--provides it with the fuel for genius. The man and the woman, while playing their traditional, stereotypical roles to perfection, also happen to be the first of their sexes. As a result, Adam and Eve are able to play out the battle of the sexes unhampered by the cultural baggage of gender politics (including those of Twain’s era, more than a century ago). They are freed from blame for their behavior (at least for a time), and as a result we the readers are relieved of our cultural responsibility to blame them. After all, it was their types that became stereotypical, not them. And so Eve and her reluctant mate emerge as so much more than mere caricatures. Instead, they can be seen and enjoyed as two young people in a new, uneasy relationship, not only with each other but with the universe and their creator. Adam’s exasperation with (or obliviousness to) Eve’s complexity, which would thud rather than resonate in any well-heeled romance, only strengthens our desire to see Eve win him over. And she does--at a price.<br />As this edition's jacket comments reminds us, the Diaries represent the only instance in Twain’s prolific career where he narrates in a woman’s voice. And I can honestly say that Eve is one of the most compellingly sweet and touchingly complex female voices I’ve ever read. This book is a tiny gem, and well worth a trip to the library or bookstore.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7306093333078024045" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10800350622020284085" style="display:none">A light humorous account of the first meeting of a man and a woman in the garden of Eden which had me smiling half way though and laughing outloud at some moments. Did you think they were bored just because they were the only two people on Earth? You coudn't be wrong. Twain nails the common, misunderstandings men and women have about each other. This is a love story, have no doubt about that, the first love story of all and also one of discovering, adapting to, and appreciating, which are crutial steps in any relationship and in life. It's a pleaseure to set in their self-discovery journey, sprinled with couple issues, care for their children, and curiosity towards everything around them. I would be given it five stars were it not for the fact that at some points I find the image of Eve a bit constrained and constrainning as it is supposed to be the reflection of all women and the mirror where the future women should look to. That's the danger of reading classics with a 21th century mind. Blame it on me. Let's not overthink it. It's pure fun.</span>

<div id="review_1351737926" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1351737926" />
    <a title="Donna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6217162-donna"><img alt="Donna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430955688p2/6217162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1351737926?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Donna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Donna" href="/user/show/6217162-donna">Donna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22675040-the-diaries-of-adam-and-eve">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6217162-donna?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6217162-donna?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1351737926" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14434371599452225012">I haven't read anything by Mark Twain since high school, which was many yesterdays ago. And I had only read his full length novels back then, not any of his humorous short stories. So I'm very glad I started with this book as an introduction to them because I really enjoyed it. It's actually two short stories in one, Adam's Diary and Eve's Diary, published separately during the first decade of the twentieth century, but included in one volume here. <br/><br/>These days, it's not uncommon to find books wit</span>
  <span id="freeText14434371599452225012" style="display:none">I haven't read anything by Mark Twain since high school, which was many yesterdays ago. And I had only read his full length novels back then, not any of his humorous short stories. So I'm very glad I started with this book as an introduction to them because I really enjoyed it. It's actually two short stories in one, Adam's Diary and Eve's Diary, published separately during the first decade of the twentieth century, but included in one volume here. <br /><br />These days, it's not uncommon to find books with multiple viewpoints in various sections, telling the same story from a different perspective, making it seem like a whole different story, depending on who's telling it. But in the early twentieth century, I doubt it was the usual thing to tell the same story twice, especially not when the characters were biblical ones. Though it wasn't until 1996 that the diaries first appeared side by side, despite the author wishing for it during his lifetime. The two diaries play off one another, putting a whole different spin on a story everyone thinks they must know. Of course, this story isn't nonfiction, though who's to say it wasn't exactly as Mark Twain wrote it?<br /><br />Fully illustrated, this book tells the tale of when Adam first met Eve, and when Eve first met Adam--not the same story at all. Up until Eve dropped in, Adam had enjoyed a world that was predictable and orderly, a mostly silent world, other than the thoughts rolling around in his head which tended to be mundane. Then one day, this creature arrived to turn his world upside down with her chattering and her projects, including the naming of every object she encountered, before Adam could even get a word in, himself. Days passed in perplexity and annoyance, with much grumbling from Adam who wished he still had the world to himself. But would this attitude last as the years wore on and he came to know Eve better? You must read the book to find out. <br /><br />Meanwhile, Eve gets her chance to tell her side of the story in the second half of this book. This section was very beautiful, not only because of the complex and delicate illustrations accompanying it, but for its content, both humorous and heartbreaking. Twain captured Eve's voice perfectly as that of the quintessential female. And I don't mean as a stereotype as much as the essence of what it is to be a woman, with Eve making her way through the world using her intelligence, curiosity, and compassion to guide her. Her view of the world was simple, yet deep, as she struggled to understand it and the other experiment she named Man. But Adam didn't seem to rejoice in her existence as she did in his. If only he would talk with her so she could understand him better. But she believed he wouldn't do that for fear she would find him lacking in intelligence. I couldn't help laughing at the digs that Eve got in about Adam when thinking about him in all innocence. But she was complimentary to him in her thoughts, as well, accepting him with all his faults because he was masculine and hers. <br /><br />There were many favorite parts of mine in this book, most of them falling in the second half in Eve's Diary. I won't spoil anything by going into detail. But I'll say my favorite parts involved Eve at the looking pool, Eve with the animals who served as her companions, and Eve waiting for the moon to return. There was another favorite scene that involved Adam, coming at the very end of the book. It was accompanied by a touching illustration and an amazing last line. No, I won't say anything more. Read the book to find out what it was that left a lump in my throat. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14434371599452225012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_815728478" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/815728478" />
    <a title="Julia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1777325-julia"><img alt="Julia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413646019p2/1777325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/815728478?book_show_action=true">Jan 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julia" href="/user/show/1777325-julia">Julia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486460307" href="/book/show/2405846.The_Diaries_of_Adam_and_Eve_and_Other_Stories">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1777325-julia?shelf=literature-fiction">literature-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer815728478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer995892853258931238">This comment from a reviewer speaks to my feelings for this slim volume:<br/><br/>"<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1244.Mark_Twain' title='Mark Twain' rel='nofollow noopener'>Mark Twain</a> first wrote the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19114808.Extracts_from_Adam_s_Diary' title='Extracts from Adam's Diary by Mark Twain' rel='nofollow noopener'>Extracts from Adam's Diary</a> in 1904, and then <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5519394.Eve_s_Diary' title='Eve's Diary by Mark Twain' rel='nofollow noopener'>Eve's Diary</a> on 1905. Both pieces were brought together in book format in 1906. It seems that Adam was based on Twain himself, and Eve on his wife, Livy, who died in June 1904, right before he wrote the Diaries. They are seen as a posthumous love-letter to Livy, so you can tell the work is very personal, and has nothing to do with Twain’s religious belie</span>
  <span id="freeText995892853258931238" style="display:none">This comment from a reviewer speaks to my feelings for this slim volume:<br /><br />"<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1244.Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">Mark Twain</a> first wrote the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19114808.Extracts_from_Adam_s_Diary" title="Extracts from Adam's Diary by Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">Extracts from Adam's Diary</a> in 1904, and then <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5519394.Eve_s_Diary" title="Eve's Diary by Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">Eve's Diary</a> on 1905. Both pieces were brought together in book format in 1906. It seems that Adam was based on Twain himself, and Eve on his wife, Livy, who died in June 1904, right before he wrote the Diaries. They are seen as a posthumous love-letter to Livy, so you can tell the work is very personal, and has nothing to do with Twain’s religious beliefs, and most certainly should not be judged for the author’s use of stereotypes of women and men. It’s nothing but a sweet, humorous, ironic and lighthearted account of the first man and woman on Earth, of how they discovered each other, perceived each other, and grew to treasure each other’s presence more than they ever treasured the Garden of Eden."<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.allfantasyworlds.com/2013/06/short-story-recommendation-giveaway.html">http://www.allfantasyworlds.com/2013/...</a><br /><br />The afterword in my copy, by Don E. Roberts, is a touching reminder that this slim volume is really a posthumous love letter to his wife, Livy. Their story is a wonder in itself, and I want to learn more about them. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/90553.William_Dean_Howells" title="William Dean Howells" rel="nofollow noopener">William Dean Howells</a>, in his book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1000962.My_Mark_Twain" title="My Mark Twain by William Dean Howells" rel="nofollow noopener">My Mark Twain</a>, has this beautiful description of Livy (Olivia)--who always called her husband "Youth". Howells says: "I make bold to speak of the love between them, because without it I could not make him known to others as he was to me....She was in a way the loveliest person I have ever seen, the gentlest, the kindest...He was a youth to the end of his days, the heart of a boy with the head of a sage."<br /><br />Livy died in 1904, and Twain wrote to his brother: "I am a man without a country. Wherever Livy was, that was my country." This certainly echoes Adam's last line in the book: "Wherever she was, THERE was Eden." So while I still enjoyed the chuckles of this small volume, it has become much more poignant to me as I learned more of the love of Twain for his wife. <br /><br />Yes, this is a quick read, and funny, and all that. To me, it's much more. Eve's words, after they are driven from the garden, reflect Twain's own outcry: "What had we done? We meant no harm. We were ignorant and did as any other children might do....We did not know right from wrong--how should we know? We could not, without the Moral Sense; it was not possible. If we had been given the Moral Sense first--ah, that would have been fairer, that would have been kinder. Then we should be to blame if we disobeyed. But to say to us poor ignorant children words which we could not understand and then punish us because we did not do as we were told--ah, how can that be justified."<br /><br />That cry would be echoed throughout time by other writers, especially the Existentialists.</span>
  <a data-text-id="995892853258931238" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2474570931" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2474570931" />
    <a title="Florencia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia"><img alt="Florencia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601866797p2/21575078.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2474570931?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Florencia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Florencia" href="/user/show/21575078-florencia">Florencia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=funny-and-ish">funny-and-ish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21575078-florencia?shelf=diaries-memoirs-letters">diaries-memoirs-letters</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2474570931" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1611636044640659503"><b>Saturday night</b><br/>Began reading.<br/><blockquote>Wednesday <br/><br/>Built me a shelter against the rain, but could not have it to myself in peace. The new creature intruded. When I tried to put it out it shed water out of the holes it looks with, and wiped it away with the back of its paws, and made a noise such as some of the other animals make when they are in distress. I wish it would not talk; it is always talking. That sounds like a cheap fling at the poor creature, a slur; but I do not mean it so. I have never heard th</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText1611636044640659503" style="display:none"><b>Saturday night</b><br />Began reading.<br /><blockquote>Wednesday <br /><br />Built me a shelter against the rain, but could not have it to myself in peace. The new creature intruded. When I tried to put it out it shed water out of the holes it looks with, and wiped it away with the back of its paws, and made a noise such as some of the other animals make when they are in distress. I wish it would not talk; it is always talking. That sounds like a cheap fling at the poor creature, a slur; but I do not mean it so. I have never heard the human voice before, and any new and strange sound intruding itself here upon the solemn hush of these dreaming solitudes offends my ear and seems a false note. And this new sound is so close to me; it is right at my shoulder, right at my ear, first on one side and then on the other, and I am used only to sounds that are more or less distant from me.</blockquote><br />It's reciprocal, Adam, or "water coming out of the places in her face that she looks out of" fella who ate the fruit of knowledge but kept talking like a moron. <br /><br />*<br /><br /><b>Sunday afternoon</b><br />Moron, indeed. It took him 10 years to appreciate her value. Such patience, woman. I'd have been out of there at the first sign of condescending sneer. "So you don't like my company, huh. I'm crushed. All righty then; farewell (you pretentious son of a... dust)." <br /><b>
  <i>It is better to be alone than unwelcome.</i>
</b><br /><br />*<br /><br /><b>Later, that Sunday</b><br />Eve: <b>
  <i>During the last day or two I have taken all the work of naming things off his hands, and this has been a great relief to him, for he has no gift in that line, and is evidently very grateful.</i>
</b><br /><br />Adam:<b>
  <i>I get no chance to name anything myself. The new creature names everything that comes along, before I can get in a protest.</i>
</b> <br /><br /><s>2018</s> 2019: Our communication skills remain intact. <br /><br />*<br /><br /><b>Later, that Sunday...</b><br />These diaries, sheer irony, I'd like to think - good ol' vexing irony. But it could be too dangerous for the macho's little brain that's still clinging to the Middle Ages. The man may actually buy it.<br /><br />*<br /><br /><b>Midnight</b><br />After finishing Adam's diaries, this was a 2.5 star book. Eve's musings changed my mind - though the "love despite abuse" reference is abominable in any century.<br /><br />*<br /><br /><b>Monday</b><br />Recalling Eve's thoughts.<br /><blockquote>Friday<br />[...] By watching, I know that the stars are not going to last. I have seen some of the best ones melt and run down the sky. Since one can melt, they can all melt; since they can all melt, they can all melt the same night. That sorrow will come—I know it.</blockquote><br /><br /><br />July 30, 18<br />* Later on <a href="https://nonbookreviews.wordpress.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">my blog.</a></span>

<div id="review_28791061" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/28791061" />
    <a title="Lori" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/369169-lori"><img alt="Lori" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601772635p2/369169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/28791061?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lori" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lori" href="/user/show/369169-lori">Lori</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/369169-lori?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer28791061" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16000401015386037669">Bought this at the used book store today, and read in the car between grabbing lunch and christmas shopping. <br/><br/>I haven't read Twain since middle school; I have a hazy recollection of Huckleberry Finn and Tom Sawyer getting into mischief. I was not sure what to expect, but what I expected was far inferior to what I read. <br/><br/>Twain breathes life into Adam and Eve in a way that I've known only one other author to do (David Maine - in his biblical fiction novels). <br/><br/>Beginning the day after her creation, we </span>
  <span id="freeText16000401015386037669" style="display:none">Bought this at the used book store today, and read in the car between grabbing lunch and christmas shopping. <br /><br />I haven't read Twain since middle school; I have a hazy recollection of Huckleberry Finn and Tom Sawyer getting into mischief. I was not sure what to expect, but what I expected was far inferior to what I read. <br /><br />Twain breathes life into Adam and Eve in a way that I've known only one other author to do (David Maine - in his biblical fiction novels). <br /><br />Beginning the day after her creation, we follow the diaries of Eve and of Adam. Filled with confusion, curiosity, and a wickedly innocent need to discover and explore, Eve is introduced as the brighter, more aggressive half. She has the ability to talk to and tame the wild animals, and gives names to everything in sight. In contrast, we see Adam, lounging in the tree branches, hiding from the talkative Eve, creating shelter from the weather as she frollicks among the flowers. <br /><br />Of course, we follow their stories to the Tree of Knowledge, watch as their Garden becomes a savage, unsafe land, and share in their separate reactions to the birth of their first child, Cain.<br /><br />While Twain's idea of modern woman came across in this collection of diary entries - she being known to annoy Adam with her endless chatter, falling into the pond after staring at her own reflection, being sensitive to Adam's moods and wanting to do things to please him - it was done in a tastefully, harmlessly, almost humorously.<br /><br />I will certainly be adding more Twain to my to-be-read shelves now. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16000401015386037669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3421877663" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3421877663" />
    <a title="Sheila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/74924009-sheila"><img alt="Sheila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593072464p2/74924009.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

  <span id="freeText11586587290949096912" style="display:none"><b>I read this while listening to the original audiobook with the same title by Mark Twain on Spotify (links attached). Go check it out!</b><br /><br />This is my first time reading (and finished) a book by a classic author (my first one was Pride and Prejudice and I determine to win and get through the journey! Maybe later!), and well aww I'll just well I <b>RECOMMEND THIS TO EVERYBODY! YOU! YES, YOU! GO READ IT!</b> It's available on Project Gutenberg (links attached).<br /><br /><i>What's so good about this,</i> you may ask?<br />Well. I had a hard time reading classics. This is a perfect start for me. The language is not too hard to understand, though there are quite many vocabulary that when I searched on the dictionary it says, <i>"archaic English"</i> or old-fashioned English. Welp. That's Classic, yall! I guess!<br />I've always known that Mark Twain was famous for his witty, funny stories. And it proves me right! I literally laughed here and there.<br />It was also a very good depiction of "man vs woman" narratives and point of views, written based on Mr. Twain himself and his wife. At times I was like, <i>"bruh did guys really think of girls THAT way?", "girls don't talk THAT much... Right?"</i>, and another times I was like, <i>"Eve is SOOO relatable!".</i><br />I can also see how different man and woman are, started very deep on their nature and perspectives. A very subtle way to show it, in my opinion, is how Adam's Diary is : short, solid, and clear. On the other hand, let's just say Eve has her own way of in explaining with a very good skill of using words.<br /><br />In conclusion : I've attached some links that you may find helpful, so <b>go go go! read this! Why are you still here?</b><br /><br />Links<br />The Diaries of Adam and Eve by Mark Twain on Spotify <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://open.spotify.com/album/2c9bbIq34J4dProXO6O4aI?si=AsjGuwogTQq_hlVN4pnm_A">https://open.spotify.com/album/2c9bbI...</a><br />Eve's Diary, Complete by Mark Twain <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/8525">https://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/8525</a><br />Extracts from Adam's Diary, translated from the original ms. by Mark Twain <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/1892">https://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/1892</a><br /><br />You can also get both and find many others short story here : The $30,000 Bequest, and Other Stories by Mark Twain <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/142">https://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/142</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7944066842202996510">An absolute fun filled with interesting reflections (on gender, Moral Sense, God's attitude, etc).<br/>I laughed very loud as I was reading the book and was underlining the passages that I liked. But then I gave up the underlining later. Otherwise would have underlined the entire book. A short book and can be read in a sitting. And I advise you not to miss it.<br/><br/></span>

<div id="review_2239774232" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2239774232" />
    <a title="Elise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4922926-elise"><img alt="Elise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491356414p2/4922926.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2239774232?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elise" href="/user/show/4922926-elise">Elise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2239774232" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4872061905335166529">This is some of Mark Twain's most beautiful writing (especially the sections from Eve's point of view), but the tale is still imprinted with Twain's signature wry humor, skepticism, and criticism of the human condition. I loved it so much I will have to teach it. This one will give us so much to discuss.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QxHTTlLpymBPlaXs4VC/OXn5zwe7B+x89KbMvhS/VZzbjOqn7OxiSGg8F7531k13gQAWOlo40lkjIvxUwdODzQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('SJGDJPU77W63hBg4JcS6I5bheeZDGO/6wSMVteRUwanQDLrNSz5FRpAtqmqzQkhtbhig26In0d8WpyVfMTgX+A==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1244.Mark_Twain">About Mark Twain</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1244.Mark_Twain"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1322103868p3/1244.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1244.Mark_Twain">Mark Twain
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
15,321

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4192056109358671731"><b>Samuel Langhorne Clemens</b>, better known by his pen name <b>Mark Twain</b>, was an American author and humorist. He is noted for his novels <i>Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</i> (1885), called "the Great American Novel", and <i>The Adventures of Tom Sawyer</i> (1876).<br/><br/>Twain grew up in Hannibal, Missouri, which would later provide the setting for <i>Huckleberry Finn</i> and <i>Tom Sawyer</i>. He apprenticed with a printer. He also work</span>
  <span id="freeText4192056109358671731" style="display:none"><b>Samuel Langhorne Clemens</b>, better known by his pen name <b>Mark Twain</b>, was an American author and humorist. He is noted for his novels <i>Adventures of Huckleberry Finn</i> (1885), called "the Great American Novel", and <i>The Adventures of Tom Sawyer</i> (1876).<br /><br />Twain grew up in Hannibal, Missouri, which would later provide the setting for <i>Huckleberry Finn</i> and <i>Tom Sawyer</i>. He apprenticed with a printer. He also worked as a typesetter and contributed articles to his older brother Orion's newspaper. After toiling as a printer in various cities, he became a master riverboat pilot on the Mississippi River, before heading west to join Orion. He was a failure at gold mining, so he next turned to journalism. While a reporter, he wrote a humorous story, "The Celebrated Jumping Frog of Calaveras County," which proved to be very popular and brought him nationwide attention. His travelogues were also well-received. Twain had found his calling.<br /><br />He achieved great success as a writer and public speaker. His wit and satire earned praise from critics and peers, and he was a friend to presidents, artists, industrialists, and European royalty.<br /><br />However, he lacked financial acumen. Though he made a great deal of money from his writings and lectures, he squandered it on various ventures, in particular the Paige Compositor, and was forced to declare bankruptcy. With the help of Henry Huttleston Rogers, however, he eventually overcame his financial troubles. Twain worked hard to ensure that all of his creditors were paid in full, even though his bankruptcy had relieved him of the legal responsibility.<br /><br />Born during a visit by Halley's Comet, he died on its return. He was lauded as the "greatest American humorist of his age", and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3535.William_Faulkner" title="William Faulkner" rel="nofollow noopener">William Faulkner</a> called Twain "the father of American literature".<br /><br /><i>Excerpted from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia</a>.</i><br /><br />AKA:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14956076.___________" title="Μαρκ Τουαίν" rel="nofollow noopener">Μαρκ Τουαίν</a> (Greek)</span>
  <a data-text-id="4192056109358671731" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
